006197806X|5.0|PANDEMONIUM is the thrilling, thoughtful continuation of Lauren Oliver's dystopian trilogy, where citizens undergo brain surgery to cure amor deliria nervosa, a disease otherwise known as \\"love.\\"<br /><br />In the first book, DELIRIUM, an uncured 17-year-old named Lena Haloway meets Alex. He seems safe; he even has the signature scarring to prove he's been cured. He hasn't, though, and the two fall for each other. He introduces her to the possibility that love might not be a disease despite society's belief to the contrary. Lena also learns about the Resistance, and the Uncureds living in the Wilds, where love is free to bloom. Lena and Alex make a plan to join them, but during a botched escape, Alex is killed.<br /><br />PANDEMONIUM picks up with Lena being cared for in the Wilds by the Invalids, a fringe group of Uncureds who fight for the social freedom to love. Out there, life is hard --- the Invalids must fight for every scrap of life, or they will starve. It is in the Wilds where Lena is reborn, transformed. The girl from DELIRIUM is no more. The new Lena is hard, a freedom fighter. She pushes thoughts of Alex out of her mind, and when she returns to civilization, she goes with a special task: watch Julien Fineman, the-soon-to-be-cured poster boy for a \\"Delirium Free America,\\" also known as the \\"DFA.\\"<br /><br />But in a sudden twist of fate, Julien and Lena are captured by Scavengers --- havoc-wreaking, anarchic Uncureds --- who raid a DFA rally. Without hope of escape, they find themselves starved and tortured, but why? The Scavengers thrive on chaos. They do not fight for a cause, like the Invalids do. So why kidnap Julien and Lena? They are without a cause and not organized. Or are they?<br /><br />Within the confines of their dark cell, a romance takes root between Julien and Lena. To Julien, Lena is just a girl, cured (she, like Alex, has the fake signature scarring), and free from disease. To Lena, Julien is the enemy. But their time together changes things, and she finds herself drawn to him, despite her love for Alex. Are her new feelings for Julien a betrayal to Alex? Lena finds herself conflicted. She does not want to grow attached to Julien, who just may not be the unwavering poster boy the DFA has made him out to be. Julien finds solace in Lena, opening up to her about his past --- a past he never had been able to share with anyone before.<br /><br />Soon enough, however, they realize that maybe it wasn't fate that threw them together, and that more complicated political machinations are behind their predicament.<br /><br />Lauren Oliver's finely crafted, fast-paced second installment is sure to thrill teenage and adult readers alike. PANDEMONIUM is as much about Lena and Julien's unlikely romance as it is about our responsibility to each other --- how sacrificing one life for the good of many could be, in some instances, one life too many. This multi-layered novel will have readers questioning and thinking, and above all, cheering for amor deliria nervosa.<br /><br />Reviewed by Jordana Frankel

1410446077|4.0|When it comes to the story of Cinderella, you're stuck with either images of dancing and singing mice, or the bloody limb version from Grimm's. CINDER, however, introduces a whole new twist on the beloved fairy tale that is sure to be something you've never read before --- and is just as exciting. There's a handsome prince, a lost shoe, a horrible stepmother, and even some semblance of a carriage, but not in the way you remember.<br /><br />Cinder spends most of her days in a booth in the market place in New Beijing fixing other people's mechanical problems. Nothing out of the ordinary ever really happens until the day Prince Kai comes in begging for his android to be fixed. Prince Kai and Cinder immediately hit it off, but Cinder knows nothing will ever come of it. There are too many obstacles in the way. For one, a deadly plague is ravaging the countryside, and Prince Kai surely has to focus on finding a cure before he could ever get involved in a relationship. There is also another little problem that Cinder is too afraid to tell Prince Kai, something that Cinder's stepmother and stepsisters won't let her forget --- Cinder is a cyborg. Part human and part robot, Cinder is an outcast in society, and there is no way royalty would ever get involved with a social reject.<br /><br />Of course, the royal ball is coming up soon, and Cinder would like to think that she could attend and perhaps even dance with Prince Kai. Things start to unravel quickly, though, and this dream goes up in smoke. One of Cinder's stepsisters contracts the plague, and Cinder is blamed. Then comes the announcement that the cunning and ruthless lunar queen is coming for a visit, and she intends to force Prince Kai into marrying her or else risk starting a war. While fixing Prince Kai's android, Cinder discovers a secret message about a long-lost lunar princess that may just bring peace between the lunar people and earth.<br /><br />You know the rest of the story, right? Cinder is visited by herfairy godmother who turns a pumpkin into a carriage, Cinder and the prince fall madly in love, Cinder runs away and leaves her shoe, and everyone ends up happily ever after.<br /><br />Think again. This is not the story you grew up with, and let's just say that there is a little more at stake than making sure the shoe fits.<br /><br />I've read some \\"re-imaginings\\" of fairy tales before, and CINDER is by far the best. Marissa Meyer manages to use a fairy tale to provide a glimpse into the future that is more realistic than you might think. It's not hard to imagine technology becoming integrated with humans, nor is it hard to realize that our world will be facing much more difficult problems in the future than we are now. For all of you Cinderella purists, though, there is no need to worry. Meyer stays as true to the story as possible, but leaves enough wiggle room to make sure that you'll be clamoring for the next book in the series.<br /><br />Reviewed by Benjamin Boche

068484267X|5.0|Frank McCourt came out of nowhere with this raucous debut, a memoir of tragedy mixed with charm. It details the period of his life from child to adolescent in Limerick, Ireland, in the muddy days of Postwar Britain. His father, a man devout to his country and the drink, instills in him an appreciation of the world and its temptations; and his pious mother does her best to teach him values and gratuity. These all combine to make McCourt's literary voice, which is once refreshingly original and carefully contemplative of the tragedies that are continually visited upon the family within a few short years. Any other writer would make this the most depressing book in recent publication history, but in his hands it is palatable, wise, and oftentimes very funny. McCourt's childhood seems light years apart from what most people have today, but there is still a very human optimism and self-sufficiency throughout the keeps the book from wallowing in despair. The end result is a memoir that, while perhaps more imagination than fact, shows resiliency and a wry enjoyment of life that is stripped bare of ann incidentals. The book's success is a no-brainer- its appeal is wide and deep. Recommended for all modern lit fans.

He talks about the different kinds of \\"nothing\\" (i.e., empty space vs. lack of even space vs. lack of anything including the laws of physics). And then he completely fails to deliver. B005LAIGPU|5.0|When I first heard about this film, I thought that it was simply another cheap \\"found footage\\" attempt at the superhero genre about three kids who get superpowers and one of them is mentally unstable.<br /><br />This is why, generally, marketing sucks.<br /><br />Thus at first I ignored it, not really caring until I saw statements from comic-book creators and others whose opinions I really respect calling it a great film. This piqued my curiosity. I wondered why so many people were really enjoying this film. I finally got around to seeing it this past weekend, and I can say wholeheartedly that this is an excellent film that is more in the vein of the great \\"found footage\\" film genre, like CLOVERFIELD, THE BLAIR WITCH PROJECT, and THE LAST EXORCISM rather than the lackluster attempts such as QUARANTINE, the PARANORMAL ACTIVITY franchise and most of the associated horror films it spawned.<br /><br />The way that I see the film is as a great entry into the superhero genre, relating to characters like Spider-Man and The X-Men. What if three high-school boys got superpowers, but didn't have the moral compass of a character like Professor X or Uncle Ben?<br /><br />The film is the brainchild of director Josh Trank and his co-writer Max Landis (son of the once-great John Landis), and it embraces the tropes of superhero cinema and the \\"found footage\\" conceit and it turns both ideas on their heads. While most of the film is shot from one point of view, as their telekinetic powers grow, they're able to move the camera to unnatural POV shots rather than just keeping it static. And especially the third act of the film begins to use several different cameras, such as camera phones, security cameras, ATM cameras and police surveillance cameras splicing them all together to get a greater perspective and sense of cinematic urgency that films of this type generally lack. The special effects of the film are crucial and very rarely do they not feel seamless. Trank also gives the control of the in-film camera to the most important character rather than let it change hands terribly often or give it to a secondary character. This allows us to watch the evolution of the character's arc and give it a more organic feel.<br /><br />The film also features powerful performances by Dane DeHaan as the increasingly isolated and troubled Andrew and Alex Russell as his conflicted cousin Matt. The only issue that I have with the film, and it's a pretty minor quibble, is that the dialogue sometimes doesn't feel natural, and I think that's more of an issue of restraint rather than the actors playing it badly or the writing being poor.<br /><br />When all is said and done, the film is very clever, very well-paced (it's very brief, running at around 80 minutes), and very well-acted. It puts a very refreshing spin on a genre we're becoming maybe a little too familiar with and helps breathe new life into it. It really is the first great film of the year.ol of the in-film camera to the most important character rather than let it change hands terribly often or give it to a secondary character. B007K3JCAE|4.0|The lives and partnership of Sherlock Holmes and Dr. John Watson have been retooled, played with, reimagined and played very straight throughout their long cinematic career; from films like Billy Wilder's [[ASIN:B00005JKHF The Private Life of Sherlock Holmes]], which explores the possibilites of the Holmes/Watson partnership, and [[ASIN:B005ETAMHS The Seven-Per-Cent Solution (Universal Vault Series)]], which deals with Holmes' more addictive nature, and [[ASIN:B0000AUHPC Young Sherlock Holmes]], from Chris Columbus, who directed the first two HARRY POTTER films, and of course the fantastic PBS series [[ASIN:B004132HZS Sherlock: Season One]] from Steven Moffat, who's made an incredibly engaging modernizaton, Holmes and Watson are not the inviolate characters that purists would like us have them be. Guy Ritchie's big-budget big-screen treatments of their adventures, with the first self-titled film, and now with A GAME OF SHADOWS, handle the character deftly but with a wink in his eye and a manic spring in his step rather than the traditional visage of a frown, a magnifying glass and a hunting cap. This not-entirely-alternate vision of the character has more to do with the casting of Robert Downey Jr. as the world's greatest detective of the Victorian age as much as anything else. Downey's Holmes is constantly exercising his mind with new devices, new disguises and new mysteries as well as constantly abusing his body with various chemicals (in this film, we see him drinking embalming fluid early on). He's also doing everything he can to insinuate himself in the life of his only friend and partner, the erstwhile and begrudgingly faithful Dr. Watson, who is also given new life by Jude Law as more of a masculine and independent man of action rather than his more common interpretation of the portly sycophant hanging on Holmes' every word. Law's Watson would, at times, rather be as far away as possible from Holmes as possible than being his eternally loyal, eunuch-like companion.<br /><br />With A GAME OF SHADOWS, we are introduced (beyond cryptic references in the first film) to Professor James Moriarty (played perhaps too stone-faced by Jared Harris), nicknamed The Napoleon of Crime and famously Holmes' arch-enemy. At the film's opening, according to Holmes' massive investigation, has already started a plan that looks to throw all of Europe into chaos and war. The endgame, or course, is unseen. Meanwhile, Watson readies to wed his fiancee Mary (Kelly Reilly, reprising her role) and Holmes and his Secret Service brother Mycroft (played with relish by Stephen Fry) intend to take him to his bachelor party. Naturally, the place they end up going to has an entirely different purpose for Holmes than it does for Watson. He investigates a gypsy fortune-teller (Noomi Rapace, the Swedish actress who played Lisbeth Salander in the original MILLENNIUM trilogy, as well as the star of the upcoming sci-fi epic PROMETHEUS) who Moriarty has targeted for murder. She, like Irene Adler (also reprised here in a much smaller role by Rachel McAdams), becomes a reluctant part of the investigation after being saved by Holmes. However the stakes change again for Holmes, who was more self-serving in the investigation rather than personally invested, when Moriarty commits a heinous act that wounds Holmes more deeply than he lets on, but also announces his intention to make Watson and his new bride \\"collateral damage\\" in their game. This, and the other atrocity that Moriarty committed, force Holmes to take more proactive steps to protect the Watsons. They travel from London to Paris to Geneva, discovering new information, gathering new allies and facing new enemies as the labrynthine plot of Moriarty begins to unravel, until it all culminates at a peace summit at Reichenbach Falls, a place famous in the lore of the mental and physical combat of Holmes and Moriarty.<br /><br />The super slow-mo techniques used in the first film to display Holmes' combat acumen is utilized here with an exciting unpredictability since there are occasionally other players at work whose assistance can be unexpected, and makes it as enjoyable if not more so than the first film. Also the same can be said for his myriad glances at seemingly insignificant items and showing how they play out later. The set-pieces are terrific, such as the fight at the bachelor party, the train sequence, and the arms factory. The major set piece, much like the warehouse explosion in the first film now takes place in a dark forest with several pursuers and a very VERY big cannon, and it's terrific, but Ritchie's technical expertise was never in question. He knows how to make a film look good.<br /><br />The problems I do have with the film are mostly in the performance category. Jared Harris has never been one of my favorite actors, and he plays Moriarty mostly as a cold fish, whose placid take on the character is not one of any flamboyance or real menace, with the exception of one scene when he sings along to a Schubert opera as he tortures Holmes. Noomi Rapace is criminally wasted here for such a volatile and vibrant performer. It's not her fault; her part is just terribly underwritten and she does a lot with it, but it's like she's hardly there. Also, Eddie Marsan as the inherently inept Inpector Lestrade is, with the exception of one 30-second scene, is completely absent from this film; a sad outcome for someone who is a vital part of the Holmes mythology and was such a vital part of the previous film.<br /><br />The other problem that I have is with the climactic confrontation between Holmes and Moriarty at Reichenbach Falls. I understand what they did in the context of the film; it's more of a war between minds than it is a war between bodies. However, there isn't any kind of sense of satisfaction that the whole film would be leading up to a major climactic physical confrontation. After all, they are both physically adept and, at least in common lore, they are both master swordsmen. It would have been nice to get something more out of the ending. Perhaps screenwriters Kieran and Michele Mulroney (having only one independent film to their names) were a little out of their depth on a major studio outing.<br /><br />Sorry... one last problem: The film seems far too short. There are scenes that either could have been shortened or entirely cut in order to make room for scenes of greater humor, drama, or character. The film ends too abruptly and it seems like it could have been at least fifteen minutes longer.<br /><br />However, the scenes that shine brightest are the scenes between Downey and Law. They have a great, effortless chemistry that translates to an easily believable friendship on the screen. Fry is terrific fun as well, and McAdams continues to play Irene Adler with a flirty yet deadly side, although her role in this film is greatly diminished.<br /><br />Downey, as per usual, is terrific as Holmes. He enjoys playing this role, and that's evident. He's extremely clever as a performer and plays Sherlock Holmes with a vitality and bawdiness never seen before. Law as Watson is equally good as his frustration with Holmes' antics builds to blind rage at moments, all the while deeply caring about Holmes as his friend and always feels the need to stand by him.<br /><br />All in all, this is a very solid 3.5 out of 5 star film, and although it lacks the same punch as the first film, I'd still stand in line to see Downey and Law be Holmes and Watson again.s utilized here with an exciting unpredictability since there are occasionally other players at work whose assistance can be unexpected, and makes it as enjoyable if not more so than the first film. Also the same can be said for his myriad glances at seemingly insignificant items and showing how they play out later. The set-pieces are terrific, such as the fight at the bachelor party, the train sequence, and the arms factory. The major set piece, much like the warehouse explosion in the first film now takes place in a dark forest with several pursuers and a very VERY big cannon, and it's terrific, but Ritchie's technical expertise was never in question. He knows how to make a film look good.<br /><br />The problems I do have with the film are mostly in the performance category. Jared Harris has never been one of my favorite actors, and he plays Moriarty mostly as a cold fish, whose placid take on the character is not one of any flamboyance or real menace, with the exception of one scene when he sings along to a Schubert opera as he tortures Holmes. Noomi Rapace is criminally wasted here for such a volatile and vibrant performer. It's not her fault; her part is just terribly underwritten and she does a lot with it, but it's like she's hardly there. Also, Eddie Marsan as the inherently inept Inpector Lestrade is, with the exception of one 30-second scene, is completely absent from this film; a sad outcome for someone who is a vital part of the Holmes mythology and was such a vital part of the previous film.<br /><br />The other problem that I have is with the climactic confrontation between Holmes and Moriarty at Reichenbach Falls. I understand what they did in the context of the film; it's more of a war between minds than it is a war between bodies. However, there isn't any kind of sense of satisfaction that the whole film would be leading up to a major climactic physical confrontation. After all, they are both physically adept and, at least in common lore, they areboth master swordsmen. It would have been nice to get something more out of the ending. Perhaps screenwriters Kieran and Michele Mulroney (having only one independent film to their names) were a little out of their depth on a major studio outing.<br /><br />Sorry... one last problem: The film seems far too short. There are scenes that either could have been shortened or entirely cut in order to make room for scenes of greater humor, drama, or character. The film ends too abruptly and it seems like it could have been at least fifteen minutes longer.<br /><br />However, the scenes that shine brightest are the scenes between Downey and Law. They have a great, effortless chemistry that translates to an easily believable friendship on the screen. Fry is terrific fun as well, and McAdams continues to play Irene Adler with a flirty yet deadly side, although her role in this film is greatly diminished.<br /><br />Downey, as per usual, is terrific as Holmes. He enjoys playing this role, and that's evident. He's extremely clever as a performer and plays Sherlock Holmes with a vitality and bawdiness never seen before. Law as Watson is equally good as his frustration with Holmes' antics builds to blind rage at moments, all the while deeply caring about Holmes as his friend and always feels the need to stand by him.<br /><br />All in all, this is a very solid 3.5 out of 5 star film, and although it lacks the same punch as the first film, I'd still stand in line to see Downey and Law be Holmes and Watson again.

0525423656|4.0|A utopia is always hiding a dystopia. Beneath all that perfect living and niceness is corruption and evil like you have never known before. Sadly, a utopia is just a pipe dream. In the Matched trilogy, Ally Condie started with a Giver-esque utopia that gave way to the dystopia hiding beneath. Now, with Crossed, you see what was really going on behind the scenes in the Society's utopia.<br /><br />Cassia was matched to Xander, but she fell in love with Ky. Sent to a work camp in the edge of the provinces, she could only think of escaping to find Ky. As an Aberration, an outcast of the Society, Ky was sent to act as a decoy for the enemy in one of the outermost villages. That way the Enemy only killed outcasts, not real citizens of the Society. When Ky gets the chance to run, he takes a young boy and the only friend he has made at the village and heads for the canyons. When Cassia sees an opportunity to get out of the camp where she is held, she takes it and ends up at one of the outer villages used as decoys. Once there, she and Indie, another girl from the camp, pick up and head the direction of the canyons where they assume Ky will be.<br /><br />Along the way through the canyon, both groups make a lot of mistakes and realizations. It is clear there are things even their cynicism wouldn't lead them to have believed the Society was capable of. When Cassia, poisoned by the tablet the Society makes as a \\"lifesaver\\", refuses to stop moving, her determination not only saves her life from the poison, but also leads her straight to Ky. What she doesn't expect when she finds him is for the two of them to have hugely different paths to take for the future. Cassia is drawn to the resistance, in the hopes of stopping the Society, but Ky knows how damaging the resistance can be for those who are involved in it. They have finally found each other, but will they be able to stay together?<br /><br />This was not your average sequel because the story has taken such an enormous turn from the original novel. While the original novel was much more a fallen utopia, the dystopia in this book rings loud and clear. It is hard to miss, actually! The Society is such an evil, oppressive society and all you want is to see them taken down. In fact, while I can see Ky's side of things, I have to agree with Indie and Cassia- I would totally want to join the resistance. Although this sequel was a complete turn-around from the original novel, it still felt like a set-up for the final story. The action isn't enthralling, but it is certainly interesting. I think the final novel in the trilogy will be the best story yet.<br /><br />This is a great story for anyone who liked the Giver or any other young adult dystopia. It isn't too graphic or harsh, as are frequently the complaints about dystopias and post-apocalyptic stories in the YA genre. Instead, Condie prefers to let the grim future speak for itself and the oppressive government's actions leave much to the imagination. This is a great trilogy, but I am anxious to see how it ends now. The drastic switch in direction will surely make you feel the same way!n from the original novel. B007ATHNJA|5.0|This is one of the greatest of all operas and one of the most problematic. Opera as an art form was less than fifty years old when the ageing and terminally ill Monteverdi and the rakish librettist Busenello (and possibly others) staged this morally lax, sexual political bedroom romp full of ambition driven people who appear to have been totally lacking in virtue or have carefully hidden any trace. \\"Deh,nasconditi, o Virtu'\\" (1643 Teatro SS Giovanni e Paolo, Venice). Claudio Monteverdi died a few months after the opening. There are several conflicting manuscripts of this opera but from later dates (1651). This production uses a new edition of the score by Jonathan Cable. No other information is given<br />I have several versions of this opera both DVDs and CDs and each has some particular outstanding trait or character. For Seneca it's Robert Lloyd in the Maria Ewing DVD but her Poppea is too sentimental. For Ottavia it's Anne Sofie von Otter in the John Eliot Gardiner CD who's lament is truly heart rending but the vehemence and hate in her order to Ottone to kill Popea is frightening. But the two characters who really matter in this opera are Nero and Poppea. The Nero here is Philippe Jaroussky and I think the best of the lot. He brings to this very nasty, self-serving character an air of innate rotteness that comes across in his alto counter-tenor but still gives off enough 17-keto steroids in the voice to let us know that this is a man. His voice has a sneering quality to it and it blends perfectly with that quality in the great star Danielle de Niese. In her previous disc of this role with Emmanuelle Haim conducting and Alice Coote as Nero there was certainly fire and sensuality but a bit too much sliding of notes and rubato of tempo for me. In this disc she is more precise in tone and tempo and I might add she becomes the Poppea of the times. She has mastered nuance and covered deceit wonderfully well. This Poppea can turn her back on a former lover and is now possessed by ambition as was Anna Bolena to come. Nothing matters but to become Empress at any cost. Danielle's stage presence is remarkable. She walks on a crowded stage and no one else is there. She sings with her body as well conveying an unique ability to demand the attention of everybody. She is a wonder to behold.<br />The Ottavia of Anna Bonitatibus was too old and stiff. Her lament was a disappointment. The Seneca of Antonio Abete was also dull. However the two nurses, Arnalta & Nutrice, and their description of the perils of old age were great. Venice opera at the time had comic parts mixed with dramatic in a presentation. Thus the nurses were played by old men in drag using falsetto. Needless to say the music of Les Arts Florissants with William Christie was right on. What a pleasure to listen to those antique instruments really sing! Pier Luigi Pizzi did the costumes as in the the first DVD I own. Here he is much more austere than the lush extravagance of the previous recording. In all the costumes fit the action more closely although sometimes it look like they had borrowed the dining room drapes. This recording is a great success. Even if you already have several DVDs or CDs invest in this one. Highly recommended.possessed by ambition as was Anna Bolena to come. Nothing matters but to become Empress at any cost. 0439023513|4.0|This was a great ending to a great trilogy, but there were a couple of issues that bothered me while reading this.<br /><br />First, as mentioned in my reviews of the first two books in this trilogy, the style and unfolding of the plot was a bit juvenile. This hasn't changed here. This can be contrasted with the Harry Potter series, where there seems to be a slight but distinct increase in sophistication from each book to the next, almost as though accounting for the increasing ages of the readers as they progress in the series. In this trilogy, I feel as though there is no real growth in sophistication. Even worse, I feel as though the bold, brash, shiny new \\"veneer\\" that dazzled us in the first book in the series with its outrageously engaging plot has now worn off somewhat and that makes the underlying juvenile aspect of the plot all the more visible. (To give just one example, I find that the rebel \\"army\\" is portrayed extremely ineptly to the point where it's completely unrealistic, where leaders would make stupid decisions on the whim of a stubborn girl!)<br /><br />The second problem I have with the book is the unfolding of the plot vis--vis time and details. I feel as though there will be much effort spent on one part of the story, with much detail and explanation (sometimes almost too much so, in a spoonfeeding sense), but then other times weeks and months will go by, battles will be won, and there will be no real expounding on this. Rather than describe battle scenes, the author seems to shy away from such intimidating (to the author?) detail, with the result being that much of the plot is so distantly removed from the battlefront that battles seem to transpire in the background over the course of a mere paragraph or two. This ends up giving short shrift to what seems to be an important component of the story--or, at the very least, a part of the story that could be quite entertaining.<br /><br />Finally, I was disappointed in the growth of the protagonist herself. Katniss, despite the major events in her life forcing her to grow up and face her new world, still seems to be the same somewhat immature, somewhat stubborn girl in many senses. You'd think that after all this time and all these experiences, she would finally move on from some of those silly nave thoughts and inclinations, especially in regard to relationships, both romantic and platonic, and from some of the juvenile moralizing that she often does. But, no.<br /><br />Despite these setbacks, it was still a well-rounded end to the trilogy, wrapping it up nicely, and still grabbing the reader's attention throughout.

B007DIQC90|4.0|I discovered this band listening to a free stream of their album and it caught my attention pretty quickly. They've been compared to Arcade Fire and I think that's a fair comparison. However, I also find that there's a thumping or driving nature to some of their music that reminds me of recent songs by The Head and the Heart. Lastly, their harmonies and catchy melodies remind me of The New Pornographers. The female singer sounds like a less-yodel-y version of Dolores O'Riordan from the Cranberries. There's a male-only series of songs on here that sometimes take on a Fleet Foxes kind of vocal (although not muted the way the production of their songs are).<br /><br />Comparisons aside, this is a collection of catchy songs with no dud in the bunch. It's not quite a 5-star album for me, but it's really solid. Some of the choruses are memorable, but the album really shines more so through good musicianship and good melodies. There are fewer hooks than some power-pop bands, but the album maintains a consist set of melodies throughout and that makes it a keeper. The album's first two tracks, \\"Dirty Paws\\" and \\"King and Lionheart\\" each have a well-paced and energetic feel to them and are stand-outs. Sixth track \\"Little Talks\\" features alternating male and female vocals and the most memorable chorus of the lot. \\"Six Weeks\\" follows and when paired with \\"Little Talks\\" there's a buoyant and delicious middle to the album. The ride to the end is still wonderful, made especially so with the sorrowful \\"Your Bones\\" and the sweet \\"Lakehouse\\".<br /><br />Iceland has produced much interesting music over the years, but this is the most welcome export I've encountered so far. The animal theme is carried throughout which really makes this feel like a complete album.

B000002UB6|5.0|You gotta love an underdog.<br /><br />Though perhaps a misnomer to speak of any Beatles record in such a way, 1970's Let It Be nonetheless fails to resonate with many of the Beatles faithful in the same way that their other albums do.  Arguments range from the loose, essentially live playing style sounding sloppy and underproduced to Spector  unnecessarily excluding certain tracks from the final product while overproducing others. Won't someone make up their mind?  Whatever the reason, Let It Be is often casually dismissed by those who felt that the album failed to reach the heights of the greatest rock records ever recorded.  I suppose when you grew up during a time when 67's 'Sgt. Peppers' was followed a little over a year later by The White Album and then Abbey Road in '69, one's expectations are somewhat skewed.<br /><br />Perhaps it's because I came from a different generation that I feel a profound sense of attachment to this record.  As a child of the 80's, I was bombarded with mascara and artificial noise which was poorly disguised as popular music.  That I sought solace in Thrash Metal and Jim Croce (I'm seeking counseling) was perhaps indicative of the fact that, at my core, I craved honest, organic music.  Let It Be has that in spades. The brainchild of Paul, the 'Get Back' sessions were intended to unite the four men who changed the world under a single banner by forcing them to be a band again.  Indeed, from the microphone John and Paul share on 'Two of Us' to John's incessant joking which bookends several tracks, Let It Be sounds like an album recorded under the best possible set of circumstances.  Sure, history has taught us otherwise but therein lies the problem.  Courses are taught on the history of this band at Universities.  It's simply impossible for some to disregard the fact that the Beatles shelved these songs in favor of Abbey Road, or separate the implosion of this band from Let It Be.  However, taken on its own merits the plaintive, appropriately bluesy feel that permeates Let It Be showcases a band that was, in the end, still the best band in the world.<br /><br />Abandoning many of the studio techniques Martin and The Beatles pioneered, Let It Be approaches the recording of an album in the same way the band did with every album up through Rubber Soul.  Clearly adoring Richards and the Stones by this point, Lennon and Harrison in particular are gloriously vulnerable on \\"Across The Universe\\" and \\"I, Me, Mine\\" respectively.  The former, a holdover from the Abbey Road sessions and partly inspired by his first wife's \\"nagging\\", is a piece of infinite cosmic dispassion in which Lennon repeatedly declares: \\"Nothing's gonna change my world\\".  Ironically, by the time Let It Be was released, something had.  The later, a Harrison track which restores the tempo of the record, juxtaposes Billy Preston's dreamy organ and an acoustic guitar in the verses with Harrison's almost gristly, amplified electric in the chorus'.  Though Harrison was right that his best work never got a fair shake from Paul or John ( My Sweet Lord should have been a Beatles song), his selfishly titled offering during the Get Back sessions is a \\"second rate\\" song for the ages.<br /><br />Joining the seemingly inexhaustive list of deep Beatles tracks, 'Dig a Pony' and 'One After 909' are any other band's singles.  John, ever his own worst critic, would dismiss 'Pony' in later years as one of his throw away songs.  While his nonsensical lyrics in the verses may be just that, John unleashes the full range of his voice on the chorus, climaxing in a falsetto that touches the end of his range before cascading back down to open the next verse. 'One After 909' is fun in the way 'Twist & Shout' or even 'Obladi Oblada' are fun and is nothing more than a pleasure to listen to.  A relative relic by Beatles' standards (this track dates back to the early 60's), where it may have gotten lost as an odd duck on the pop-heavy 'A Hard Day's Night', here the up-beat Blues track feels right at home.  Nothing too fancy, just a well placed smile before Paul's second ballad on the record.<br /><br />\\"And now we'd like to do: All The Angels Come\\".  Only The Beatles could get away with introducing Paul's greatest gift to rock with John wasting tape.  And yet somehow only The Beatles could have made it work.  John's introduction to Let It Be so disarms the listener that the instantly recognizable opening piano chords take the listener by surprise and conjour the memories and emotions each of us has attached to this song over the past forty years.  Yes, Harrison's guitar solo on the album is inferior to the single version but the integrity of Paul's achingly expressive vocal and Preston's warm organ accompaniment is not compromised in the least.  Dropped almost haphazardly in the first half of the album, 'Let It Be' was destined to become the Beatles last opus and, had they known that, maybe it would have been moved to the end of the record and without John's foolishness.  Thank God they didn't.<br /><br />Still in that vein, Let It Be can be thought of as an album of inclusions and exclusions.  John's \\"Don't Let Me Down\\", though released as a single, certainly had a home on this album.  Spector's orchestral and choir arrangements did not.  Easily the most legitimate and universal complaint with this album, Paul rightly removed much of Spector's meddling on the 2003 \\"Naked\\" release.  'The long and winding road', with its orchestral and choir arrangement, has the effect of placing a late 60's/early 70's time stamp on a track that needed no flourishes.  And yet, having heard this version for so many years, the \\"Naked\\" version can sound sparse in comparisson.  Yes, I'm going to have it both ways.  Where this album succeeds in additions/flourishes is the inclusion of Billy Preston.  His organ playing is such an integral part of Let It Be (and later on Harrison's solo records) that it's no wonder there was a legitimate conversation about making him the 5th Beatle.<br /><br />Of course they didn't and Let It Be served as The Beatles' curtain call.  Blame the strength of Plastic Ono Band, McCartney's eponymous debut and All Things Must Pass. Had those records flopped Let It Be might have been little more than a brief lay over; the 7th inning stretch for a band that released music the way rabbits do what rabbits do.  No one would have begrudged The Beatles a break, except maybe the band members themselves.  The only thing The Beatles may have had in greater supply than brilliant songs was the energy to write and record them.  Regrettably, Let It Be suffers the fate of bearing witness to a band that just couldn't keep it together anymore.  Don't you dare call it a death rattle.  In a world of records that are artificially assembled on a computer as much as they're written and recorded, Let It Be offers a window to a time when even the greatest of studio bands could still (mostly) come together, jam and bless us with one last moment of brilliance.p-beat Blues track feels right at home. 0985114606|4.0|Gideon is a powerful, wealthy man used to getting what he wants with no questions asked.  Eva is a woman determined to lead a successful, healthy life on her own terms.  When Gideon feels a strong attraction to Eva, he wants her at any cost, even if that cost is his carefully pieced together life...<br /><br />Talk about a dysfunctional relationship.... I have to admit that I was completely absorbed in this book, despite the fact that I normally do not like hot and cold relationships.  Gideon and Eva are both victims of abuse, and when they find they are inexplicably drawn to each other, their relationship proves tumultuous, powerful, frustrating, and beautiful all at the same time.  While they both have managed to recover and be successful adults (individually), the relationship status throws each of them completely out of their element, and they both find that they are not as recovered as they would have thought.  Gideon is a very dominant male, he likes control and power.  Eva is the opposite, she is terrified of losing control.  However, Gideon's control and powerful ways melt away some of the harsh memories for Eva, and she finds herself opening up more and more, which terrifies her.  The results are a very rocky relationship, neither knowing what to do or how to cope.<br /><br />As I said, normally I do not like hot and cold romances.  However, in this case, there are REAL justifications behind the off and on again changes.  As I got to know the characters, I understood why they acted the way they did, and why they were so afraid of love.  On one hand, you want them to work their issues out, but on the other, you hope that they don't hurt each other even more by trying to make it work.  Either way, the result is an enthralling story that I did not want to put down.<br /><br />Sylvia Day does such an excellent job detailing the dynamics of this relationship, there is no way the story can be told in just one book.  But still, I was very surprised when the book ended, I was simply not ready to quit reading.  It was the type of ending that had me searching through the e-book, looking for more and sighing when I realized that I would have to wait for book 2.  The ending is not really a cliff-hanger, but the story is far from over.  So, readers be prepared to be left wanting and itching to discuss everything that you have read.<br /><br />Bared to You is not a happy, whimsical romance story.  This is a story about a relationship with a gritty, rocky road to romance, high on sexual content, and so enthralling readers won't want to put it down. B000001FSR|3.0|If you love Waits, you'll probably at least like this album. I wasn't sure what to think, and this album convinced me that I really don't like Tom Waits at all.<br /><br />Musically, the performances, arrangements, and production are more varied and more carefully thought out than some of his \\"we just hit stuff while he growled like a drunk\\" albums. But, oddly, those albums work better. Despite the spooky production, the songs are simplistic. The overall album has a theatrical intent, not as music, but to paint a picture; it's not really music you'd listen to for pleasure. Or indeed that I would listen to at all. However, you don't listen to Tom Waits for pleasure, you listen to him to inflict a certain mood on the audience, and in this he succeeds, mostly. If you're looking to capture, in an album, the feeling of \\"mediocre show tunes performed by a drunk at a low-rent theater,\\" order now.<br /><br />But, I'm not sure I can buy into the usual \\"drunk loser cosplay\\" this time. The production seems contrived; I feel like I'm watching the action from box seats with a glass of wine while whispering to friends. I am never involved in the emotion. Coltrane is a better soundtrack-for-having-nothing-left-to-lose. Nick Drake is better \\"actually I'm doing quite well but I want to feel like I could kill myself for half an hour\\" music. And Waits' other albums are generally more believable, partly because they aren't trying so hard.<br /><br />The production does project a certain depressing mood. As annoying as I found it, this self-indulgent slumming is exactly what Tom Waits fans love. However, I hate nearly every song on the album, and much of the album has a faint aura of overproduced pretension tainting the desired \\"loserness\\". It's carefully and artfully crafted, and I think that was a mistake.<br /><br />[edit] After another listen, Sorry, Tom, it's really only a 3 star album.y Waits-fan friends will like. Mostly. If you never hear music you hate, you will never hear music you love!

B000AYEL6U|5.0|Based on the book by Elizabeth Gaskell and set in the 1850s of Victorian England, North and South is a story with some strong similarities to Pride and Prejudice. After losing his faith, Margaret's preacher father moves his wife and daughter form bucolic south England to its industrial north, where he earns his living teaching. Initially the transition is hard on Margaret, but though she doesn't have Elizabeth Bennet's sense of humor, she does have her intelligence and determination, and she begins to make the best of her situation. When she first meets factory owner John Thornton she's shocked by the roughness of his actions and the haughtiness of his demeanor, but that gradually changes as she learns more about him, and about the manners and customs of the north. As in Pride and Prejudice, there's a badly botched marriage proposal and both characters ultimately have a positive effect on each other.<br /><br />North and South has four episodes and each seemed better than the last to me. 0375869026|5.0|It is difficult to write a review for this story, because I can honestly find nothing to criticize; I warn you, then, that most of this review will most likely be me gushing about August.<br /><br />I was very impressed by the writing style. Auggie, the heart of the story, is the most believable and central to each and every new perspective that is added.<br /><br />[Some spoilers will follow.]  The simple honesty and unquestioning acceptance in his voice made him a character every bit as deserving of his happy ending as any character I have ever read.  I previously read a reviewer's complaint that the entire premise of this book, the ending in particular, is self-congratulatory, for other people to feel good about the fact that while society may suck sometimes, we are ultimately accepting and inherently good--and that August is only special because of his appearance.  I don't have the same experience as everyone else, but I can't agree with this view.<br /><br />To read this book, for me, was to ultimately come to *know* August.  Although he ascribed his own standing ovation in the end as because he was different, because no one else understood what it was to be him--I didn't believe this for a second.  Reading all the additional perspectives in the book allowed me to understand firsthand just how the characters around him grew because of him.  His sister, Jack, Summer, Amos--these characters ultimately become stronger because of knowing Auggie, and making the choice to be his friend.  Being kind isn't just the throwaway phrase we sometimes use it for, or synonymous with the bland 'nice'--'Oh, you're nice, but...'--rather, it's an active choice.<br /><br />\\"Kinder than is necessary. Because it's not enough to be kind. One should be kinder than needed.\\"  August isn't just kind himself; he inspires kindness.  Although he is brutally mocked by kids his age, and even by some so-called adults, he doesn't stop going to school every day.  He perseveres for those that are brave enough to show him even the smallest bit of kindness, and returns it tenfold.  He isn't perfect.  He throws tantrums and acts younger than his age.  But he is without question the strongest character in this book.  Anyone who can say, \\"Like, it's okay.  I know I'm weird-looking, take a look, I don't bite,\\" is, in my mind, capable of quite a great bit of kindness.  He always assumes the best of people, and the most heartbreaking part of the book is when that faith in someone he thought was his friend was shattered--because it is this faith that keeps him going, that keeps him from being bitter about the cards he has been dealt.<br /><br />I warned you that I would start ranting about this book, given the chance.  But really, it says something that I'm not ranting about the book itself, but about August. B005HS00NW|4.0|The opening lines of the first track \\"Intro\\" do a marvelous job of framing the album that follows: \\"We didn't need a story. We didn't need a real world. We just had to keep walking. And we became the stories, we became the places.\\"<br /><br />HURRY UP, WE'RE DREAMING creates a symphonic landscape not just reminiscent of the 80s with all it's hyperactivity and pathos, but a complete re-imagining of that era sans its plastic, artificial, faux glamor. Everything was big in the eighties, the hair, the cars, and especially the emerging synth-pop beats from the likes of NEW ORDER, PET SHOP BOYS, and DURAN DURAN. At their best, the originators of electro-drum machines layered over keyboards and synthesizers created a musical landscape with a depth greater than the sum of their largely synthetic parts. At their worst, they managed to create music devoid of real emotional resonance, robotic sounds played by robotic musicians.<br /><br />M83 succeeds in creating a sound that deeply connects to their forebears without being swallowed by them. They take the best of what made synth-pop immensely likable and couple it with a modern sensibility. They do become the places, even the memories of the frantic eighties repainted on a new canvas. Tracks like \\"Reunion\\" practically pull the listener out of their post 9-11 modern world and immerse them in that shimmering decade. Energetic, at times even silly (Raconte - Moi Une Histoire), HURRY UP, WE'RE DREAMING is never boring.<br /><br />I disagree with the reviewers who labeled this album derivative or too mainstream. There is a significant difference between co-opting someone's work and integrating a sound into a stylistic approach that adds as much as it borrows. I'm not entirely sure what 'too mainstream' means anymore. Everything is mainstream. Even Indy has become mainstream. The bottom line should be: is the music listenable? Does it have something to say? I feel that HURRY UP, WE'RE DREAMING answers on both fronts.<br /><br />Ultimately building a successful album, not just a collection of singles, is about creating ATMOSPHERE. And M83 have done a fine job here. It's impossible (as someone who lived through the glorious and awful eighties) to listen to this album without being taken somewhere. Maybe not the real world, maybe not the eighties that was, but to a place where flowing beat and synthetic sounds chart a pathway into a musical homage to the best of what the eighties had to offer. They do become the stories, and what's even better, they take you with them.<br /><br />4/5 Starsimately building a successful album, not just a collection of singles, is about creating ATMOSPHERE. And M83 have done a fine job here. It's impossible (as someone who lived through the glorious and awful eighties) to listen to this album without being taken somewhere. Maybe not the real world, maybe not the eighties that was, but to a place where flowing beat and synthetic sounds chart a pathway into a musical homage to the best of what the eighties had to offer. They do become the stories, and what's even better, they take you with them.<br /><br />4/5 Stars

B0075564A8|5.0|What's to say about Truth Or Dare that hasn't been written already? When the documentary came out in the spring of 1991, Madonna was the most controversial artist the mainstream had seen since the glory days of Elvis. I think it's easy to forget in 2012 that back in the late 80s/early 90s, Madonna's choice of subject matter was quite taboo. The sexual nature of her early work frightened a lot of people, but it also made her undeniably interesting. Sexually charged themes aside, the film works because it's a sneak peek into the life of a woman in charge. In charge of her show. In charge of her ever-shifting image. And most importantly, in charge of her career.<br /><br />I first saw Truth Or Dare in my early teens and fell in love with it. This wasn't merely because of Madonna, either. The supporting \\"cast\\" is top notch. Nikki and Donna are super endearing as M's faithful backup singers. The personalities of the dancers shine through as well, with the exception Gabriel and Kevin. Even Madonna's run-ins with her family are classic and humorous. \\"Oh God, I was so afraid to do Like A Virgin!\\" she exclaims at one point, just before her dad visits her backstage. She's vulnerable here. There's Madonna without makeup, blowing her nose, admitting that she isn't the best singer, working through her relationship with Warren Beatty, and sometimes even forgetting the cameras are watching.<br /><br />My favorite part of Truth Or Dare is the concert editing. The movie opens with a full blast performance of \\"Express Yourself.\\" Oliver's reunion with his estranged father is painted by a backdrop of \\"Oh Father.\\" Madonna defending her controversial show against the Vatican is accompanied by \\"Live To Tell.\\" The refreshing arrival in Europe is paired with a full-color \\"Holiday.\\" And, of course, the end of the film reminds you to remember your roots with \\"Keep It Together.\\"<br /><br />I also think that Blond Ambition is, to date, Madonna's best tour. Confessions is a close second, but here is Madonna at her popularity peak. Anyway, I'm rambling now. If you haven't seen it, watch Truth Or Dare to be reminded or educated on why Madonna is the world's best-selling female artist. What she stands for. Who she is.<br /><br />Favorite Madonna line (in response to Bobby Brown's 1990 arrest for his onstage antics): \\"I hope he's in jail when I get there.\\"

0439023513|5.0|**This is a review essay I had to write for my English class, and I have to post it on a site such as this one.<br /><br /> Mockingjay is the third book in the Hunger Games trilogy and is set far into the future, after war and famine have taken much of the United States.  This book focuses on the ongoing battle between the rebels and the Capitol.  Katniss Everdeen, the victor of the 74th Annual Hunger Games, has become a major key in the plot to overthrow the government.  Her mockingjay pin has become a symbol of the rebellion, and she herself adopts the title after she learns of the impact she can have.<br />This book is structured in such a way that time goes by in months in just three pages.  Instead of focusing on the downtime between conflicts, Collins prefers to keep the reader interested in the suspense than in minute details that won't affect the story.  The introduction of so many new characters was hard to follow at first, but as each of these characters were given back stories, they were easier to understand.<br />Katniss' character becomes a web, and the reader has the opportunity to follow along during her paranoid moments and her realizations.  Instead of learning anything new about her character as the reader would in the first two books, her personality grows.  The reader sees what her present will do for her future.  The third book concentrates more on other people's stories, mainly other Hunger Game victors.  The reader learns exactly how cruel the Capitol is based on these stories.  Katniss is not the only victim of the Capitol, but her constant defiance makes her a target.  The rebels use her for propaganda to gain supporters for their plot to overthrow the crooked president.<br />The climax is difficult to find at a first glance because the second book gives the major climax of the series.  This book explains how Katniss and the other rebels take their country back after constant threats from the Capitol.  Katniss' realization of the rebel army's equally crooked president changes everything in this book.  She is no longer a pretty face used to gain supporters.  She becomes a warrior intent on revenge and justice.  She wants her country back, but she does it for all of the people of Panem, the country that was once the United States.  The action leading up to this point continues until Katniss make a final decision of how to resolve her issue.<br />Katniss became a face of hope the people who had been forced to live in poverty for too long.  Her bravery and instincts make her a role model for others who want to live in a free country.  Her decisions not only affect her but thousands of others.  Though her confusion of who to trust makes her conscience fragile, she picks herself up and makes the biggest decision of her life, and possibly the biggest decision for Panem thus far.  Her decision symbolizes a different kind of hope, a hope that the country can become whole again.  So Katniss goes from being a symbol of hope to bringing that hope to others.<br />The author's choices in structure and character development in this book support the previous two books.  She made the story action-packed and mildly easy to follow while also challenging the reader.  Her decision to reveal Katniss' backstory in the previous books helps this book flow with details of the present.  The reader can almost guess what Katniss will do throughout the story because of the way each book is set up.  The reader becomes one with the character through this understanding.  The story is developed in a way that makes the book difficult to put down until the resolution is fulfilled.  And though there is a conclusion to the series, the reader can still expect surprises in the future of the characters.  The ending might not be a night-to-day sunshine happy one, but it's more realistic.  The openness is refreshing.  The reader gets a conclusion and a look into mystery of what might happen one day.

She tells me when she put on the uniform she became an extrovert and able to speak to her patients about anything.<br /><br />Like me, she craves solitude to recharge.<br /><br />At one point in her book Susan questions whether introversion is a result of nurture or nature. Later she poses the possibility that it doesn't really matter. What matters more, she feels, is what the individual is comfortable with.<br /><br />As a writer, I am most comfortable when working with as few distractions as possible. I find a ringing telephone or even the background noise of a radio, irritating. Unfortunately, I am uncomfortable with promoting myself and my books, especially face to face. I tend to spend 95% of my promotional time online. Also unfortunately, there are times when I need to speak to people about my books face to face. So sometimes, I have to force myself to play the role of an extrovert.<br /><br />Although the author doesn't seem to think the nature nurture aspect is all that important, I find myself fascinated by it. My early childhood was spent with foster parents who often stated, \\"Children are meant to be seen and not heard.\\"  Later, upon meeting some of my birth family, it seemed to me they were pretty extroverted.<br /><br />My husband is also an introvert, yet he manages to do well in the traditionally extroverted career of sales.<br /><br />I found Quiet fascinating, informative, and self affirming. If you are an introvert, it will help you understand and accept who you are. If you have a spouse, friend, or child who is introverted, hopefully it will also help you celebrate and encourage them. B00000JLWW|5.0|Some movies become a part of you.  They are just so enrooted in your heart that despite their obvious, sometimes glaring flaws, you can't help but openly adore them.  `Titanic' is one of those movies.  It became a revelation upon its initial release back in 1997, and it continues to be considered one of the most beloved films in recent memory; maybe of all time.  It's generations `Gone with the Wind', `Titanic' is a sweeping romantic epic that has captured the hearts of millions and, as we all promised upon the film's conclusion, we have never let go.<br /><br />I subject myself to `Titanic' roughly once a year.  It is a struggle because the minute Rose leaps from that lifeboat back into the sinking ship my tear ducts begin active duty and it's rather embarrassing, especially since I can't prevent it from happening.  With the release of the 3D version of the film I took the opportunity to try crying in public, considering that I have not seen the film on the big screen (it was released when I was twelve and my parents forbid it).  I was ecstatic, as was my wife.  This is just such a BIG movie, and event in itself, and you kind of have to see it on the big screen at least once; right?  With my bulky black glasses on, I settled into my seat (directly in the center of the theater thanks to arriving thirty minutes ahead of time) and prepared myself for the adventure once more.<br /><br />It started and I literally got Goosebumps.<br /><br />As I watch `Titanic', I am not blinded to the film's faults; for there are really far too many of them too ignore.  The script is atrocious.  The dialog is ridiculous (I hate it when characters detail out large amounts of information out of cinematic necessity despite the fact that NO ONE TALKS LIKE THAT).  The acting is spotty at best (Winslet is decent in the beginning, great in the closing).  Really, the film truly excels in two areas; the technical grandeur and James Cameron's dynamic direction (yes, I'm a fan of his win, even if he is not my particular winner that year).  Watching the cast muddle through such poorly conceived conversations and watching the pathetic plot points unravel could have easily became a chore.  I mean, what other film would be forgiven such horribly mediocre offerings?<br /><br />I can't think of one; and yet it doesn't matter.<br /><br />`Titanic' works because all these elements, no matter how mediocre they can be on their own, come together in a way that creates cinematic magic.  The chemistry between the two leads is undeniable and the whole idea of their unlikely love affair surpassing tragedy and `meaning' something becomes such a strong driving force for the film that one cannot help but become emotionally wrapped up in the film, despite what number of views one is on.<br /><br />No, `Titanic' is just that one movie that gets a free pass because it is more than a movie; it is an event all its own.  The film becomes a part of you.  When Celine Dion belts out the chorus lines to `My Heart Will Go On' you can feel the emotional connection well up in your heartstrings and it's really all over from that point forward.  It doesn't make sense and it probably isn't deserved, but millions of warm blooded human beings agree; `Titanic' is one of those movies that defies logic. B002BWP4A6|5.0|This is a long - almost 2 1/2 hrs - look at life in a northeast German village, taken as a microcosm of Germany in her transition from the rigidities of the 19th to the violence of the 20th centuries.  The geographic placement is clear from the Junker system depicted, the name of the county seat (Teglitz - a Wendish name) and the presence of Polish farm laborers.  The story is told in retrospect by an old man who, in the movie, is the young schoolmaster, the one element not corrupted. The leading characters/institutions - the Baron and his wife, the doctor, the pastor, the police  - are shown as heartless and shallow; it is they, and their extensions (e.g., the Baron's son and the midwife's son), who are the targets of the sometimes lethal attacks perpetrated  by those who must live under them.  American reviewers of this movie have seen this theme and interpret it simplistically as nothing more than shoving the German nose into the dreck of Naziism yet once again - and this may indeed by part of the message.  (It would appear that throwing the Baron's son into the river and stealing his whistle might have more to do with a depiction of the SECOND dictatorship on German soil in the 20th century).  Overall it would seem that the message is rather a general one, perhaps equally applicable to the French and Russian revolutions - that a violent response to the inequities of society may lead to even greater injustice.  This village, after all - on the eastern march - would have been obliterated with the destruction of the Oder-Neisse provinces in 1945.  And yet the movie begins and ends with a chorale of \\"A Mighty Fortress\\" - at the end, with the congregation, the pastor sitting as one of them, viewed from the altar - the divine perspective. Surely this is the appropriate response to the slings and arrows of this world.

B004XJRQUQ|4.0|By now you've likely heard the gist of the first, eponymous, Hunger Games through the publicity surrounding the movie. The trilogy is enjoyable as a continuous read because of how the story evolves over the course of the novels, with the characters and events developing - at least, those who survive. While I think that the first novel was written with the possibility of a second in mind, the second was certainly written so that the third would follow quite quickly.<br /><br />I read the trilogy in just under six weeks, with some sections proceeding much more quickly and easily than others. I will say without question that the books are exciting - not only because of continually-surprising events and an often-fascinating world, but also because of Collins' pacing and timing. She makes some chapters impossible to simply set the Kindle down without continuing for just one more ... or three or four. I really have to point out that although the first-person narrative is through Katniss, who is quite an introvert and who relates much of the backstory through asides and interior conversations, Collins does a very good job of keeping the story moving. It rarely drags in the first novel, aside from one long stretch in the middle that goes a bit too long for building dramatic tension and really ends up inspiring a frustrated \\"get on with it!\\" from the reader.<br /><br />While many reviewers took away a deep sense of connection to Katniss or one of the other characters, all of whom lose so much over the course of the novels, or a breathless love story which was certainly present but just not my forte, I was most impressed by grander themes of dehumanization and the risk of drifting towards nihilism in the pursuit of power. The two conflicting societies - Capitol and District - were such an incredible contrast of color and tedium, excess and insufficiency, trivium and survivalism, that they constantly reminded one of the tension underlying this otherwise harmonious if lethal arrangement. The constant obsession on image and the cults of hero worship that continued to emerge must be a withering commentary on moral choices that value form over substance.<br /><br />There were some minor detractions from the story. One is Katniss herself - she's far too intelligent and well-spoken for one who grew up in the poorest District with schooling that prepared her for life in the coal mines. Her vocabulary is very near Collins' own, which is to say impressive, and there are times the fourth wall becomes transparent and I see not the conflicted and desperate teen but rather the author and a life experience, especially in matters of love, that brings the author's maturity in focus.<br /><br />The other is hard to explain without giving away the premise of the second novel. Suffice to say, I really do wonder if Collins had known the latter two novels would be published, if she'd add some foreshadowing into the first novel to allude to certain events that arise very quickly and completely, so much so that when they appear the reader asks \\"how did I miss that?\\" Of course, certain others in the novels missed it completely as well, and for good reasons.<br /><br />After so much lit crit, I'll say what matters most. I really enjoyed reading this trilogy.rangement. The constant obsession on image and the cults of hero worship that continued to emerge must be a withering commentary on moral choices that value form over substance.<br /><br />There were some minor detractions from the story. One is Katniss herself - she's far too intelligent and well-spoken for one who grew up in the poorest District with schooling that prepared her for life in the coal mines. I really enjoyed reading this trilogy.

0062082353|4.0|Nonfiction! What is this black magic? You all must be thinking that I'm a pretty contradictory person. I don't read books about dragons. I love James Maxey's GREATSHADOW. I don't read anthologies. I love ARMORED by John Joseph Adams. Now I'm going to tell you that I don't read nonfiction. Ever. But it turns out that I really liked AMERICAN SNIPER written by Navy SEAL Chris Kyle, with Scott McEwen and Jim DeFelice. An autobiography about a Navy SEAL? Well if you're going to read nonfiction you might as well read the most exciting nonfiction available.<br /><br />Chris Kyle served as a Navy SEAL during Operation Iraqi Freedom. Kyle and his platoon were part of some of the thickest fighting the US Armed Forces were involved in, from pacifying Fallujah to cleaning out Sadr City. AMERICAN SNIPER is an unapologetic, patriotic, and personal look at the conflict in the Middle East from the perspective of a special forces sniper.<br /><br />AMERICAN SNIPER begins with Kyle's first sniper kill and then follows his path from childhood all the way through his enlistment until he leaves the Navy in 2009. Reading about the way Kyle grew up and the life he led before becoming a SEAL really gives insight into what sort of person it takes to be a special forces operator. Kyle is a legitimate cowboy, a Texan, and a \\"good ol' boy\\". He's the sort of guy that you would really like to buy a beer and it shows through his writing. The autobiography is broken up into chapters with sub-chapters, making for quick easy reading and the tone of the narrative is so laid back that it's really more like having a conversation than reading.<br /><br />The personal bits are all very compelling, Kyle's wife Taya even writes small excerpts here and there that add an even more delicate touch. The parts written from Taya's perspective are often about the toll Kyle's enlistment and service took on the family. These parts serve as a reminder that SEALs aren't just warriors. They are sons and brothers and fathers and husbands as well. But enough about the touchy-feely stuff.<br /><br />The legend of Chris Kyle is really what makes this book a must read. During his service, Kyle wracked up 160 confirmed sniper kills. This is just the confirmed kills, but the number claimed is actually 255. Kyle doesn't once brag about his tally, in fact he is completely humble about his whole experience as a SEAL. Kyle fought in Iraq, not for any medals, but to protect his country. Kyle doesn't regret any of his kills and he refuses to make apologies for what he has done. Some people are likely to be offended by the candid nature of the autobiography, but I doubt many of these people are likely to buy a book about snipers in the Iraq War to begin with.<br /><br />AMERICAN SNIPER is a great read. If you are curious about Navy SEALs and the way their operations have changed with the emergence of the War on Terror this is the book for you. This is a very personal story with no shortage of action, humor, mourning, and hope. If you have a problem with patriotism you should skip this one, but I found it to be a fun time.<br /><br />Here's a thanks to all who serve in America's Armed Forces for their service and sacrifice.<br /><br />Recommended Age: 16+<br />Language: Chris Kyle cusses like the cowboy he is but he doesn't overdo it. Still be warned.<br />Violence: Yes, sometimes morbid but never gruesome.<br />Sex: None.<br /><br />Nick Sharps<br />Elitist Book Reviewsthers and husbands as well. 0439023521|4.0|Collins introduces us into a dystopian world in which Panem, the predominant nation in North America, holds annual, brutal, bloodbaths called \\"Hunger Games\\" in order to suppress and subdue the citizenry. We see the world through the eyes of Katniss Everdeen, a tough as nails teenage girl bent on taking care of her mom and little sister by whatever means necessary. Brazenly, Katniss takes the place of her younger sister when Primrose is chosen to be a contestant in the Hunger Games. Collins then adeptly builds suspense to the breaking point at which time hell is let loose in an arena in which children and teenagers fight to the death.<br /><br />The Cons:<br />1) Collins spends a substantial length of her book describing what each contestant is or will be wearing in the Hunger Games procession. My wife enjoyed the focus on clothing and fashion. I could have done without knowing the details of what each contestant would be wearing.<br />2) The trilogy didn't end with this book. The next two novels aren't as thought out, developed, or unique.<br />3) We don't learn a lot about the other districts in Panem. If Collins had spent the time she did on everyone's wardrobes leading up to the Games on getting to know the terrain, culture, and people in the other districts I feel we would have had a much better idea of and appreciation for her world building.<br /><br />The Pros:<br />1) Character development is solid and perfectly paced<br />2) The story is suspenseful, dramatic, and adequate...no big holes or let downs in the plot itself.<br />3) Though we see the world through the eyes of Katniss, she doesn't necessarily pick up on all the things we, as readers, are able to deduce. Collins did a great thing in writing the narrative from the perspective of a teenage girl, and to the author's credit, she realistically portrays that world view. Katniss doesn't know everything, particularly when it comes to reading people and their intentions. For example, she struggles with knowing the intentions of her male friends while the readers are able to see between the lines and deduce for themselves what Katniss misses because of personality defects.<br /><br />In all \\"The Hunger Games,\\" is a very well written novel that will sufficiently pull you into a post apocalyptic and chilling world that is a reminder to us all what could happen should freedom become second to the alluring safety and strength brought by dictatorship.wing the intentions of her male friends while the readers are able to see between the lines and deduce for themselves what Katniss misses because of personality defects.<br /><br />In all \\"The Hunger Games,\\" is a very well written novel that will sufficiently pull you into a post apocalyptic and chilling world that is a reminder to us all what could happen should freedom become second to the alluring safety and strength brought by dictatorship.

1451627289|4.0|What would YOU do if you discovered a time portal that takes you back to the 9th of September 1958?<br />Perhaps a phone call to the Breevort building in New York in mid January 1959 to warn Buddy Holly not to take the plane as he leaves for that fateful last tour; a visit to the Bath Hippodrome on the evening of April 16th 1960, to let Eddie Cochran know he should tell his driver to take it easy on the accelerator : and for your good deeds you could always reward yourself with massive bets on Don `Geronimo' Jordon stripping Virgil Akins for the World Welterweight title on the 5th of December '58, then wait for the following year when Nottingham Forest beat Luton Town 2 -1 in the F.A up final - or for Oxo to win the Grand National at Aintree.<br />Jake Epping - the hero of Stephen King's latest epic has somewhat loftier intentions - live in the past for five years and wait for Friday 22nd of November 1963 to prevent the Kennedy assassination - an event thought by many to have robbed America of its innocence and helped usher in that massive PR disaster known as Vietnam and ultimately the decline of the American empire.<br />The multiple shooter/grassy knoll version has become so accepted that the possibility of a lone assassin has been virtually dismissed, but after nearly fifty years of investigation, speculation and conspiracy theories on the most public murder in history (of the world's most powerful man in front of thousands of witnesses and cameras) the 20th century's greatest crime remains a mystery.<br />So now popular belief has drifted back to the lone shooter theory and Lee Harvey Oswald once again takes centre stage in this uniquely American shadow play. With this in mind Jake first has to ascertain that Oswald really was the killer - and that he was/will be working alone.<br />King wastes little time in explaining how or why a time-tunnel discovered in the back room of a road-side diner exists and pretty much gets straight to the action. The concept is brilliantly worked out - in short, when you return from the past after changing events your arrival back is always two minutes after you left, but you will have aged by however long you spent in the past. And if you re-enter the porthole history reverts back to its original state and whatever changes you made have to be made again. Also, `the past is obdurate' and doesn't like to be changed so obstacles galore will stack up against you. But also `the past harmonises' and if you look for it fate will help you. Get it? Don't worry - just read it - it will all become clear (ish). A couple of dummy runs where Jake has to start his quest all over again are a frustrating illustration of just how delicate a matter changing the future can be.<br />Once again King's forte as a creator of page turners is in evidence and I almost didn't want this book to end. The attention to detail - the amplified taste of additive-free food, the constant odour of oil refinery's polluting Texan air, the convoluted styling of automobiles, attitudes to race, sex and religion - all transport the reader as seamlessly as this story's fissure in time as Jake lives his life in early 60's America and awaits his date with destiny. I could have quite happily followed Jake Epping all the way back to the future. King's treatment of what is essentially a fantastical tale is treated in such an offhand manner that the SF element is all but forgotten and that you have to remind yourself that the book is fiction.<br />11.22.63 will have you drawing your own conclusions about what might have been if Kennedy survived: a happier, warless world? My guess is a that a series of Hoover leaked Clinton/Lewinski-esque sex scandals may have toppled Camelot - the rubble on which LBJ would still have stood and led the charge into Saigon.<br />And Buddy and Eddie? Maybe Holly would have gone on to have his dreamed of collaboration with Ray Charles and perhaps discovered and signed a young Bob Dylan to his new label whilst ona night out with his young wife in nearby Bleeker Street. Eddie would have got his close friend Gene Vincent into rehab, became an important producer and even made some respectable appearances as an actor in many late 60's counter culture movies. But history has shown, almost without exception, that pop stars create their best work early on in their careers and either fade away and/or become parodies of themselves. The same could be said for a lot of writers, but for many 11.22.63 will be considered among Stephen King's best.a night out with his young wife in nearby Bleeker Street. The same could be said for a lot of writers, but for many 11.22.63 will be considered among Stephen King's best.
